				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Reformed" and "Reformed church" redirect here. For other uses of Reformed, see <a href="/wiki/Reform_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Reform (disambiguation)">Reform (disambiguation)</a>. For Reformed churches originating in continental Europe, see <a href="/wiki/Continental_Reformed_church" title="Continental Reformed church">Continental Reformed church</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the theology of John Calvin himself, see <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_John_Calvin" title="Theology of John Calvin">Theology of John Calvin</a>.</div>

<p><b>Calvinism</b> (also called the <b>Reformed tradition</b>, <b>Reformed Christianity</b>, <b>Reformed Protestantism</b>, <b>Reformed faith</b>, or the <b><a href="/wiki/Covenant_theology" title="Covenant theology">Covenant Belief</a></b> is a major branch of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> that follows the theological tradition and forms of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> practice of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation-era</a> <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformers" title="Protestant Reformers">theologians</a>.</p>
<p>Calvinists broke from the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> in the 16th century. Calvinism differs from <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutherans</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Real_presence_of_Christ_in_the_Eucharist" title="Real presence of Christ in the Eucharist">real presence of Christ in the Eucharist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regulative_principle_of_worship" title="Regulative principle of worship">theories of worship</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Law_and_Gospel#Lutheran_and_Reformed_differences" title="Law and Gospel">use of God's law for believers</a>, among other things.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-muller_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muller-2">[2]</a></sup> As declared in the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Confession">Westminster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Helvetic_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Helvetic Confession">Second Helvetic</a> confessions, the core doctrines are predestination and election. Calvinism favored rich and wealthy as leaders sanctioned by scripture. A basic principle is that they deny the Catholic teaching that humans can achieve salvation through their actions.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The protestant part of this reformation was that considering Bible is to be interpreted by itself, meaning the parts that are harder to understand are examined in the light of other passages where the Bible is more explicit on the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The term <i>Calvinism</i> can be misleading, because the religious tradition which it denotes has always been diverse, with a wide range of influences rather than a single founder. The movement was first called <i>Calvinism</i> by Lutherans who opposed it referring to French reformer John Calvin, and many within the tradition would prefer to use the word <i>Reformed</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuller2004130_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuller2004130-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early influential Reformed theologians include <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_Zwingli" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulrich Zwingli">Ulrich Zwingli</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bucer" title="Martin Bucer">Martin Bucer</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Farel" title="William Farel">William Farel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Bullinger" title="Heinrich Bullinger">Heinrich Bullinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Martyr_Vermigli" title="Peter Martyr Vermigli">Peter Martyr Vermigli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Beza" title="Theodore Beza">Theodore Beza</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>. In the twentieth century, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Kuyper" title="Abraham Kuyper">Abraham Kuyper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herman_Bavinck" title="Herman Bavinck">Herman Bavinck</a>, <a href="/wiki/B._B._Warfield" title="B. B. Warfield">B. B. Warfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._Gresham_Machen" class="mw-redirect" title="J. Gresham Machen">J. Gresham Machen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Barth" title="Karl Barth">Karl Barth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martyn_Lloyd-Jones" title="Martyn Lloyd-Jones">Martyn Lloyd-Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Van_Til" title="Cornelius Van Til">Cornelius Van Til</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Clark" title="Gordon Clark">Gordon Clark</a> were influential. Contemporary Reformed theologians include <a href="/wiki/J._I._Packer" title="J. I. Packer">J. I. Packer</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_F._MacArthur" title="John F. MacArthur">John MacArthur</a>, <a href="/wiki/R._C._Sproul" title="R. C. Sproul">R. C. Sproul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_J._Keller" class="mw-redirect" title="Timothy J. Keller">Timothy J. Keller</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Piper_(theologian)" title="John Piper (theologian)">John Piper</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_F._Wells" title="David F. Wells">David Wells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Horton_(theologian)" title="Michael Horton (theologian)">Michael Horton</a>.</p>
<p>Reformed churches may exercise several forms of <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_polity" title="Ecclesiastical polity">ecclesiastical polity</a>; most are <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">presbyterian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Congregationalist_polity" title="Congregationalist polity">congregationalist</a>, though some are <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopalian</a>. Calvinism is largely represented by <a href="/wiki/Continental_Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental Reformed">Continental Reformed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Congregationalist_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregationalist church">Congregationalist</a> traditions. The biggest Reformed association is the <a href="/wiki/World_Communion_of_Reformed_Churches" title="World Communion of Reformed Churches">World Communion of Reformed Churches</a> with more than 80 million members in 211 member denominations around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> There are more conservative Reformed federations such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Reformed_Fellowship" title="World Reformed Fellowship">World Reformed Fellowship</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_of_Reformed_Churches" title="International Conference of Reformed Churches">International Conference of Reformed Churches</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Reformed_denominations" title="List of Reformed denominations">independent churches</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Calvinism is named after <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>. It was first used by a Lutheran theologian in 1552. It was a common practice of the Catholic Church to name what they perceived to be heresy after its founder. Nevertheless, the term first came out of Lutheran circles. Calvin denounced the designation himself:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>They could attach us no greater insult than this word, Calvinism. It is not hard to guess where such a deadly hatred comes from that they hold against me.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>John Calvin, Leçons ou commentaires et expositions sur les Revelations du prophete Jeremie 1565<sup id="cite_ref-Cottret2003_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cottret2003-10">[10]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Despite its negative connotation, this designation became increasingly popular in order to distinguish Calvinists from Lutherans and from newer Protestant branches that emerged later. Even though the vast majority of churches that trace back their history to Calvin (including Presbyterians, Congregationalists, and a row of other Calvinist churches) do not use it themselves, since the designation "Reformed" is more generally accepted and preferred, especially in the <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">English-speaking world</a>. Moreover, these churches claim to be—in accordance with John Calvin's own words—"renewed accordingly with the true order of gospel".</p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Arminian_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Arminian controversy">Arminian controversy</a>, the Reformed tradition—as a branch of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> distinguished from Lutheranism—divided into two separate groups, <a href="/wiki/Arminians" class="mw-redirect" title="Arminians">Arminians</a> and Calvinists.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> However, it is now rare to call Arminians a part of the Reformed tradition, as many see these two schools of thought as opposed, making the terms <i>Calvinist</i> and <i>Reformed</i> synonymous. While the Reformed theological tradition addresses all of the traditional topics of Christian theology, the word <i>Calvinism</i> is sometimes used to refer to particular Calvinist views on <a href="/wiki/Christian_soteriology" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian soteriology">soteriology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a>, which are summarized in part by the <a href="/wiki/Calvinism#Five_points_of_Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Five Points of Calvinism</a>. Some have also argued that Calvinism as a whole stresses the <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">sovereignty</a> or rule of God in all things including salvation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Calvinism" title="History of Calvinism">History of Calvinism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geneva_Cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Geneva_Cathedral.jpg/170px-Geneva_Cathedral.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Geneva_Cathedral.jpg/255px-Geneva_Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Geneva_Cathedral.jpg/340px-Geneva_Cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geneva_Cathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calvin preached at <a href="/wiki/St._Pierre_Cathedral" title="St. Pierre Cathedral">St. Pierre Cathedral</a>, the main church in Geneva</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CalvinInstitutio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/CalvinInstitutio.jpg/170px-CalvinInstitutio.jpg" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/CalvinInstitutio.jpg/255px-CalvinInstitutio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/CalvinInstitutio.jpg/340px-CalvinInstitutio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2307" data-file-height="3648" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CalvinInstitutio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calvin's <a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">magnum opus</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Institutes_of_the_Christian_Religion" title="Institutes of the Christian Religion">Institutio Christianae religionis</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>First-generation Reformed theologians include <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> (1484–1531), <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bucer" title="Martin Bucer">Martin Bucer</a> (1491–1551), <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Capito" title="Wolfgang Capito">Wolfgang Capito</a> (1478–1541), <a href="/wiki/John_Oecolampadius" class="mw-redirect" title="John Oecolampadius">John Oecolampadius</a> (1482–1531), and <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Farel" class="mw-redirect" title="Guillaume Farel">Guillaume Farel</a> (1489–1565). These reformers came from diverse academic backgrounds, but later distinctions within Reformed theology can already be detected in their thought, especially the priority of <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">scripture</a> as a source of authority. Scripture was also viewed as a unified whole, which led to a <a href="/wiki/Covenantal_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenantal theology">covenantal theology</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sacraments" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacraments">sacraments</a> of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Supper" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord's Supper">Lord's Supper</a> as visible signs of the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_grace" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenant of grace">covenant of grace</a>. Another Reformed distinctive present in these theologians was their denial of the bodily <a href="/wiki/Real_presence_of_Christ_in_the_Eucharist" title="Real presence of Christ in the Eucharist">presence of Christ in the Lord's supper</a>. Each of these theologians also understood salvation to be by grace alone, and affirmed a doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Unconditional_election" title="Unconditional election">particular election</a> (the teaching that some people are chosen by God for <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a>). <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> and his successor <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" class="mw-redirect" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a> were undoubtedly significant influences on these theologians, and to a larger extent later Reformed theologians. The doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Justification_by_faith_alone" class="mw-redirect" title="Justification by faith alone">justification by faith alone</a> was a direct inheritance from Luther.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuller2004131.E2.80.93132_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuller2004131.E2.80.93132-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> (1509–64), <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Bullinger" title="Heinrich Bullinger">Heinrich Bullinger</a> (1504–75), <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Musculus" title="Wolfgang Musculus">Wolfgang Musculus</a> (1497–1563), <a href="/wiki/Peter_Martyr_Vermigli" title="Peter Martyr Vermigli">Peter Martyr Vermigli</a> (1500–62), and <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Hyperius" title="Andreas Hyperius">Andreas Hyperius</a> (1511–64) belong to the second generation of Reformed theologians. Calvin's <i><a href="/wiki/Institutes_of_the_Christian_Religion" title="Institutes of the Christian Religion">Institutes of the Christian Religion</a></i> (1536–59) was one of the most influential theologies of the era.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuller2004132_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuller2004132-14">[14]</a></sup> Toward the middle of the 16th century, the Reformed began to commit their beliefs to <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">confessions of faith</a>, which would shape the future definition of the Reformed faith. The 1549 <i><a href="/wiki/Consensus_Tigurinus" title="Consensus Tigurinus">Consensus Tigurinus</a></i> brought together those who followed Zwingli and Bullinger's <a href="/wiki/Memorialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Memorialist">memorialist</a> theology of the Lord's supper, which taught that the supper simply serves as a reminder of Christ's death, and Calvin's view that the supper serves as a <a href="/wiki/Means_of_grace" title="Means of grace">means of grace</a> with Christ actually present, though spiritually rather than bodily. The document demonstrates the diversity as well as unity in early Reformed theology. The remainder of the 16th century saw an explosion of confessional activity. The stability and breadth of Reformed theology during this period stand in marked contrast to the bitter controversy experienced by Lutherans prior to the 1579 <a href="/wiki/Formula_of_Concord" title="Formula of Concord">Formula of Concord</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuller2004135_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuller2004135-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to Calvin's missionary work in France, his programme of reform eventually reached the French-speaking provinces of the Netherlands. Calvinism was adopted in the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_the_Palatinate" class="mw-redirect" title="Electorate of the Palatinate">Electorate of the Palatinate</a> under <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Elector_Palatine" title="Frederick III, Elector Palatine">Frederick III</a>, which led to the formulation of the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_Catechism" title="Heidelberg Catechism">Heidelberg Catechism</a> in 1563, and in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a> by <a href="/wiki/Jeanne_d%27Albret" title="Jeanne d'Albret">Jeanne d'Albret</a>. This and the <a href="/wiki/Belgic_Confession" title="Belgic Confession">Belgic Confession</a> were adopted as confessional standards in the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Emden" title="Synod of Emden">first synod</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Dutch Reformed Church</a> in 1571. Leading divines, either Calvinist or those sympathetic to Calvinism, settled in England (Martin Bucer, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Martyr_Vermigli" title="Peter Martyr Vermigli">Peter Martyr</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jan_%C5%81aski" title="Jan Łaski">Jan Łaski</a>) and Scotland (<a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>). During the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, the Calvinistic <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a> produced the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Confession">Westminster Confession</a>, which became the confessional standard for <a href="/wiki/Presbyterians" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterians">Presbyterians</a> in the English-speaking world. Having established itself in Europe, the movement continued to spread to other parts of the world including North America, South Africa, and Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Calvin did not live to see the foundation of his work grow into an international movement; but his death allowed his ideas to break out of their city of origin, to succeed far beyond their borders, and to establish their own distinct character.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spread">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_(south_nave),_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_%28south_nave%29%2C_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg/220px-Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_%28south_nave%29%2C_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_%28south_nave%29%2C_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg/330px-Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_%28south_nave%29%2C_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_%28south_nave%29%2C_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg/440px-Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_%28south_nave%29%2C_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3276" data-file-height="2735" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_the_Oude_kerk_in_Amsterdam_(south_nave),_by_Emanuel_de_Witte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calvinism has been known at times for its simple, unadorned churches and lifestyles, as depicted in this painting by <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_de_Witte" title="Emanuel de Witte">Emanuel de Witte</a> c.1661</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although much of Calvin's work was in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, his publications spread his ideas of a <i>correctly</i> Reformed church to many parts of Europe. In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, some cantons are still Reformed and some are Catholic. Calvinism became the theological system of the majority in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> (see <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> (see <a href="/wiki/William_Ames" title="William Ames">William Ames</a>, <a href="/wiki/T._J._Frelinghuysen" class="mw-redirect" title="T. J. Frelinghuysen">T. J. Frelinghuysen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmus_%C3%A0_Brakel" title="Wilhelmus à Brakel">Wilhelmus à Brakel</a>), some communities in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>, and parts of Germany (especially these adjacent to the Netherlands) in the <a href="/wiki/Palatinate_(region)" title="Palatinate (region)">Palatinate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kassel" title="Kassel">Kassel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lippe" class="mw-redirect" title="Lippe">Lippe</a> with the likes of <a href="/wiki/Olevianus" class="mw-redirect" title="Olevianus">Olevianus</a> and his colleague <a href="/wiki/Zacharias_Ursinus" title="Zacharias Ursinus">Zacharias Ursinus</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> and the then-independent <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>, Calvinism was a significant religion. In the 16th century, the Reformation gained many supporters in Eastern Hungary and Hungarian-populated regions in Transylvania. In these parts, the Reformed nobles protected the faith. Almost all Transylvanian dukes were Reformed. Today there are about 3.5 million Hungarian Reformed people worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Reformed_Church_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Reformed_Church-18">[18]</a></sup> It was influential in France, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> before being mostly erased due to the <a href="/wiki/Counter_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter Reformation">counter-reformational activities</a> taken up by the monarch in each country. Calvinism gained some popularity in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, especially Sweden, but was rejected in favor of <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Uppsala" class="mw-redirect" title="Synod of Uppsala">Synod of Uppsala</a> in 1593.<sup id="cite_ref-Vlib.iue.it_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vlib.iue.it-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most settlers in the American Mid-Atlantic and <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> were Calvinists, including the English <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a>, the French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> and Dutch settlers of New Amsterdam (New York), and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_people" title="Ulster Scots people">Scotch-Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterians</a> of the Appalachian back country. <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">Nonconforming Protestants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_separatism" title="Ecclesiastical separatism">Separatists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_(religion)" title="Independent (religion)">Independents</a>, <a href="/wiki/17th_century_denominations_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="17th century denominations in England">English religious groups coming out of the English Civil War</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/English_dissenters" class="mw-redirect" title="English dissenters">English dissenters</a> not satisfied with the degree to which the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> had been reformed, held overwhelmingly Reformed views. They are often cited among the primary founders of the United States of America. Dutch Calvinist settlers were also the first successful European colonizers of South Africa, beginning in the 17th century, who became known as <a href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner_Calvinism" title="Afrikaner Calvinism">Afrikaners</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg/220px-Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg.png" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg/330px-Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg/440px-Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1314" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_with_historically_sizeable_Reformed_communities.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Countries with historically sizeable Reformed communities.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> was largely colonized by Calvinist settlers from <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, who were largely <a href="/wiki/Black_Loyalist" title="Black Loyalist">Black Loyalists</a>, blacks who had fought for the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American War of Independence</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Marrant" title="John Marrant">John Marrant</a> had organized a congregation there under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/Countess_of_Huntingdon%27s_Connexion" title="Countess of Huntingdon's Connexion">Huntingdon Connection</a>. Some of the largest Calvinist communions were started by 19th and 20th century <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a>. Especially large are those in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>. In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> there are 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> congregations with about 9–10 million church members, scattered in more than 100 Presbyterian denominations. In South Korea, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a> is the largest Christian denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-Chris_Meehan_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chris_Meehan-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2011 report of the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Forum" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Forum">Pew Forum</a> on Religious and Public Life estimated that members of Presbyterian or Reformed churches make up 7% of the estimated 801 million Protestants globally, or approximately 56 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_Research_Center.27s_Forum_on_Religion_and_Public_Life_21.2C_70_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Research_Center.27s_Forum_on_Religion_and_Public_Life_21.2C_70-21">[21]</a></sup> Though the broadly defined Reformed faith is much larger, as it constitutes Congregationalist (0.5%), most of the United and uniting churches (unions of different denominations) (7.2%) and most likely some of the other Protestant denominations (38.2%). All three are distinct categories from Presbyterian or Reformed (7%) in this report.</p>
<p>The Reformed family of churches is one of the largest Christian denominations. According to adherents.com the Reformed/Presbyterian/Congregational/United churches represent 75 million believers worldvide.<sup id="cite_ref-adherents.com_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherents.com-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Communion_of_Reformed_Churches" title="World Communion of Reformed Churches">World Communion of Reformed Churches</a>, which includes some <a href="/wiki/United_Churches" class="mw-redirect" title="United Churches">United Churches</a> (most of these are primarily Reformed; see <i><a href="/wiki/Uniting_and_united_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniting and united churches">Uniting and united churches</a></i> for details), has 80 million believers.<sup id="cite_ref-WCRC_History_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCRC_History-23">[23]</a></sup> WCRC is the third largest Christian communion in the world, after the Roman Catholic Church and the Eastern Orthodox Churches.<sup id="cite_ref-adherents.com_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherents.com-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many conservative Reformed churches which are strongly Calvinistic formed the <a href="/wiki/World_Reformed_Fellowship" title="World Reformed Fellowship">World Reformed Fellowship</a> which has about 70 member denominations. Most are not part of the World Communion of Reformed Churches because of its ecumenial attire. The International Conference of Reformed Churches is another conservative association.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Church_of_Tuvalu" title="Church of Tuvalu">Church of Tuvalu</a> is the only <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">officially established state church</a> in the Calvinist tradition in the world.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation_and_Scripture">Revelation and Scripture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Revelation and Scripture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/General_revelation" title="General revelation">General revelation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">Biblical inspiration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">Sola scriptura</a></div>
<p>Reformed theologians believe that God communicates knowledge of himself to people through the Word of God. People are not able to know anything about God except through this self-revelation. Speculation about anything which God has not revealed through his Word is not warranted. The knowledge people have of God is different from that which they have of anything else because God is <a href="/wiki/Infinity_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Infinity of God">infinite</a>, and finite people are incapable of comprehending an infinite being. While the knowledge revealed by God to people is never incorrect, it is also never comprehensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201018.E2.80.9320_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201018.E2.80.9320-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png/220px-Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png/330px-Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png/440px-Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png 2x" data-file-width="649" data-file-height="648" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_no_background.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The seal of the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America" title="Presbyterian Church in the United States of America">Presbyterian Church in the United States of America</a>, an early American Presbyterian church</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Reformed theologians, God's self-revelation is always through his son <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>, because Christ is the only mediator between God and people. Revelation of God through Christ comes through two basic channels. The first is <a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">providence</a>, which is God's creating and continuing to work in the world. This action of God gives everyone knowledge about God, but this knowledge is only sufficient to make people culpable for their sin; it does not include knowledge of the gospel. The second channel through which God reveals himself is <a href="/wiki/Redemption_(theology)" title="Redemption (theology)">redemption</a>, which is the gospel of <a href="/wiki/Salvation_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation (Christianity)">salvation</a> from condemnation which is punishment for sin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201022.E2.80.9323_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201022.E2.80.9323-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Reformed theology, the Word of God takes several forms. Jesus Christ himself is the Word Incarnate. The prophecies about him said to be found in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> and the ministry of the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelve Apostles">apostles</a> who saw him and communicated his message are also the Word of God. Further, the <a href="/wiki/Preaching" class="mw-redirect" title="Preaching">preaching</a> of ministers about God is the very Word of God because God is considered to be speaking through them. God also speaks through human writers in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, which is composed of texts set apart by God for self-revelation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201024.E2.80.9325_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201024.E2.80.9325-26">[26]</a></sup> Reformed theologians emphasize the Bible as a uniquely important means by which God communicates with people. People gain knowledge of God from the Bible which cannot be gained in any other way.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200112_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200112-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reformed theologians affirm that the Bible is true, but differences emerge among them over the meaning and extent of its truthfulness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201028_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201028-28">[28]</a></sup> Conservative followers of the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_theologians" class="mw-redirect" title="Princeton theologians">Princeton theologians</a> take the view that the Bible is true and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">inerrant</a>, or incapable of error or falsehood, in every place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201031_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201031-29">[29]</a></sup> This view is very similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> orthodoxy as well as modern <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelicalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarleyHodgson199477_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarleyHodgson199477-30">[30]</a></sup> Another view, influenced by the teaching of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Barth" title="Karl Barth">Karl Barth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Orthodoxy">Neo-Orthodoxy</a>, is found in the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(U.S.A.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)">Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_1967" title="Confession of 1967">Confession of 1967</a>. Those who take this view believe the Bible to be the primary source of our knowledge of God, but also that some parts of the Bible may be false, not witnesses to Christ, and not normative for today's church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201031_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201031-29">[29]</a></sup> In this view, Christ is the revelation of God, and the scriptures witness to this revelation rather than being the revelation itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200120_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200120-31">[31]</a></sup> Dawn DeVries, a professor at <a href="/wiki/Union_Presbyterian_Seminary" title="Union Presbyterian Seminary">Union Presbyterian Seminary</a>, has written that Barth's doctrine of Scripture is not capable of resolving conflicts in contemporary churches,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries2003295_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries2003295-32">[32]</a></sup> and proposed that Scripture not be thought of as the Word of God at all, but only human reports of the revealed Jesus Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries2003309_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries2003309-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Covenant">Covenant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Covenant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg/220px-Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg/330px-Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg/440px-Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1195" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordaens_Fall_of_man.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Fall of Man</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Jordaens" title="Jacob Jordaens">Jacob Jordaens</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Covenant_theology" title="Covenant theology">Covenant theology</a></div>
<p>Reformed theologians use the concept of covenant to describe the way God enters fellowship with people in history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201034.E2.80.9335_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201034.E2.80.9335-34">[34]</a></sup> The concept of covenant is so prominent in Reformed theology that Reformed theology as a whole is sometimes called "covenant theology".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001230_n._28_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001230_n._28-35">[35]</a></sup> However, sixteenth and seventeenth-century theologians developed a particular theological system called "<a href="/wiki/Covenant_theology" title="Covenant theology">covenant theology</a>" or "federal theology" which many conservative Reformed churches continue to affirm today.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201034.E2.80.9335_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201034.E2.80.9335-34">[34]</a></sup> This framework orders God's life with people primarily in two covenants: the covenant of works and the covenant of grace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201044_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201044-36">[36]</a></sup> The covenant of works is made with <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a>. The terms of the covenant are that God provides a blessed life in the garden on condition that Adam and Eve obey God's law perfectly. Because Adam and Eve broke the covenant by eating the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_fruit" title="Forbidden fruit">forbidden fruit</a>, they became subject to death and were banished from the garden. This sin was passed down to all mankind because all people are said to be in Adam as a covenantal or "federal" head. Federal theologians usually infer that Adam and Eve would have gained immortality had they obeyed perfectly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201041.E2.80.9342_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201041.E2.80.9342-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>A second covenant, called the covenant of grace, is said to have been made immediately following Adam and Eve's sin. In it, God graciously offers salvation from death on condition of faith in God. This covenant is administered in different ways throughout the Old and New Testaments, but retains the substance of being free of a requirement of perfect obedience.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201043_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201043-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through the influence of Karl Barth, many contemporary Reformed theologians have discarded the covenant of works, along with other concepts of federal theology. Barth saw the covenant of works as disconnected from Christ and the gospel, and rejected the idea that God works with people in this way. Instead, Barth argued that God always interacts with people under the covenant of grace, and that the covenant of grace is free of all conditions whatsoever. Barth's theology and that which follows him has been called "monocovenantal" as opposed to the "bi-covenantal" scheme of classical federal theology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201048_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201048-39">[39]</a></sup> Conservative contemporary Reformed theologians, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Murray_(theologian)" title="John Murray (theologian)">John Murray</a>, have also rejected the idea of covenants based on law rather than grace. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Horton_(theologian)" title="Michael Horton (theologian)">Michael Horton</a>, however, has defended the covenant of works as combining principles of law and love.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorton2011a420.E2.80.93421_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorton2011a420.E2.80.93421-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="God">God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God in Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a></div>
<p>For the most part, the Reformed tradition did not modify the medieval consensus on the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctrine of God">doctrine of God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201054_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201054-41">[41]</a></sup> God's character is described primarily using three adjectives: eternal, infinite, and unchangeable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201055_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201055-42">[42]</a></sup> Reformed theologians such as <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Guthrie" title="Shirley Guthrie">Shirley Guthrie</a> have proposed that rather than conceiving of God in terms of his attributes and freedom to do as he pleases, the doctrine of God is to be based on God's work in history and his freedom to live with and empower people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201057.E2.80.9358_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201057.E2.80.9358-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/220px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/330px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/440px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="468" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "<a href="/wiki/Shield_of_the_Trinity" title="Shield of the Trinity">Shield of the Trinity</a>" diagrams the classic doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Traditionally, Reformed theologians have also followed the medieval tradition going back to before the early church councils of <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">Nicaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a> on the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>. God is affirmed to be one God in three persons: <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">Son</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a>. The Son (Christ) is held to be eternally begotten by the Father and the <a href="/wiki/Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Procession of the Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit eternally proceeding</a> from the Father and Son.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201061.E2.80.9362_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201061.E2.80.9362-44">[44]</a></sup> However, contemporary theologians have been critical of aspects of Western views here as well. Drawing on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern</a> tradition, these Reformed theologians have proposed a "<a href="/wiki/Social_trinitarianism" title="Social trinitarianism">social trinitarianism</a>" where the persons of the Trinity only exist in their life together as persons-in-relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201061.E2.80.9362_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201061.E2.80.9362-44">[44]</a></sup> Contemporary Reformed confessions such as the <a href="/wiki/Barmen_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Barmen Confession">Barmen Confession</a> and Brief Statement of Faith of the Presbyterian Church (USA) have avoided language about the attributes of God and have emphasized his work of reconciliation and empowerment of people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie200832.E2.80.9333_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie200832.E2.80.9333-45">[45]</a></sup> Feminist theologian <a href="/wiki/Letty_Russell" class="mw-redirect" title="Letty Russell">Letty Russell</a> used the image of partnership for the persons of the Trinity. According to Russell, thinking this way encourages Christians to interact in terms of fellowship rather than reciprocity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200129_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200129-46">[46]</a></sup> Conservative Reformed theologian Michael Horton, however, has argued that social trinitarianism is untenable because it abandons the essential unity of God in favor of a community of separate beings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorton2011a298.E2.80.93299_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorton2011a298.E2.80.93299-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christ_and_atonement">Christ and atonement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Christ and atonement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">Hypostatic union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extra_calvinisticum" title="Extra calvinisticum">Extra calvinisticum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substitutionary_atonement" title="Substitutionary atonement">Substitutionary atonement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Threefold_office" title="Threefold office">Threefold office</a></div>
<p>Reformed theologians affirm the historic Christian belief that <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> is eternally <a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">one person with a divine and a human nature</a>. Reformed Christians have especially emphasized that Christ truly <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_of_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Incarnation of Christ">became human</a> so that people could be saved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200182_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200182-48">[48]</a></sup> Christ's human nature has been a point of contention between Reformed and Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>. In accord with the belief that finite humans cannot comprehend infinite divinity, Reformed theologians hold that Christ's human body cannot be in multiple locations at the same time. Because <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutherans</a> believe that Christ is bodily <a href="/wiki/Real_presence" class="mw-redirect" title="Real presence">present in the Eucharist</a>, they hold that Christ is bodily present in many locations simultaneously. For Reformed Christians, such a belief denies that Christ actually became human.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201065.E2.80.9366_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201065.E2.80.9366-49">[49]</a></sup> Some contemporary Reformed theologians have moved away from the traditional language of one person in two natures, viewing it as unintelligible to contemporary people. Instead, theologians tend to emphasize Jesus' context and particularity as a first-century Jew.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStroup1993142_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStroup1993142-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg/220px-Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg/330px-Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg/440px-Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4864" data-file-height="5900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Allegorie_van_Christus_als_redder_van_de_mensheid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This Dutch stained glass allegory shows Christ ascending the cross with Satan and several dead people on his back. Faith is personified as a woman to the right of a naked man on the ground asking Christ the way of salvation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>John Calvin and many Reformed theologians who followed him describe Christ's work of redemption in terms of <a href="/wiki/Threefold_office" title="Threefold office">three offices</a>: <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingly_office_of_Christ" title="Kingly office of Christ">king</a>. Christ is said to be a prophet in that he teaches perfect doctrine, a priest in that <a href="/wiki/Intercession_of_Christ" title="Intercession of Christ">he intercedes to the Father</a> on believers' behalf and offered himself as a sacrifice for sin, and a king in that he rules the church and fights on believers' behalf. The threefold office links the work of Christ to God's work in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Israel">ancient Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200194_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200194-51">[51]</a></sup> Many, but not all, Reformed theologians continue to make use of the threefold office as a framework because of its emphasis on the connection of Christ's work to Israel. They have, however, often reinterpreted the meaning of each of the offices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStroup1993156.E2.80.93157_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStroup1993156.E2.80.93157-52">[52]</a></sup> For example, Karl Barth interpreted Christ's prophetic office in terms of political engagement on behalf of the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStroup1993164_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStroup1993164-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christians believe <a href="/wiki/Jesus%27_death" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus' death">Jesus' death</a> and <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection</a> makes it possible for believers to attain forgiveness for sin and reconciliation with God through the <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" title="Atonement in Christianity">atonement</a>. Reformed Protestants generally subscribe to a particular view of the atonement called <a href="/wiki/Substitutionary_atonement" title="Substitutionary atonement">substitutionary atonement</a>, which explains Christ's death as a sacrificial payment for sin. Christ is believed to have died in place of the believer, who is accounted righteous as a result of this sacrificial payment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200193_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200193-54">[54]</a></sup> Contemporary Reformed theologians such as <a href="/wiki/William_Placher" title="William Placher">William Placher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Duff" class="mw-redirect" title="Nancy Duff">Nancy Duff</a> have criticized this view, claiming it makes God appear abusive or vindictive and sanctions violence by the strong against the weak.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001222_n._19_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001222_n._19-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sin">Sin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_sin" title="Christian views on sin">Christian views on sin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Total_depravity" title="Total depravity">Total depravity</a></div>
<p>In Christian theology, people are created good and in the <a href="/wiki/Image_of_God" title="Image of God">image of God</a> but have become corrupted by <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a>, which causes them to be imperfect and overly self-interested.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200166_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200166-56">[56]</a></sup> Reformed Christians, following the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, believe that this corruption of human nature was brought on by Adam and Eve's first sin, a doctrine called <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>. Reformed theologians emphasize that this sinfulness affects all of a person's nature, including their will. This view, that sin so dominates people that they are unable to avoid sin, has been called <a href="/wiki/Total_depravity" title="Total depravity">total depravity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200171.E2.80.9372_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200171.E2.80.9372-57">[57]</a></sup> In colloquial English, the term "total depravity" can be easily misunderstood to mean that people are absent of any goodness or unable to do any good. However the Reformed teaching is actually that while people continue to bear God's image and may do things that appear outwardly good, their sinful intentions affect all of their nature and actions so that they are not pleasing to God.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some contemporary theologians in the Reformed tradition, such as those associated with the PC(USA)'s Confession of 1967, have emphasized the social character of human sinfulness. These theologians have sought to bring attention to issues of environmental, economic, and political justice as areas of human life that have been affected by sin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim200173_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim200173-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvation">Salvation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Salvation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="22991" data-file-height="30000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz_van_Rijn_-_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Return_of_the_Prodigal_Son" class="mw-redirect" title="The Return of the Prodigal Son">The Return of the Prodigal Son</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Parable_of_the_Prodigal_Son" title="Parable of the Prodigal Son">Parable of the Prodigal Son</a> illustrating forgiveness</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">Sola fide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">Justification (theology)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" title="Sanctification">Sanctification</a></div>
<p>Reformed theologians, along with other Protestants, believe salvation from punishment for sin is to be given to all those who have <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity" title="Faith in Christianity">faith</a> in Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201077.E2.80.9378_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201077.E2.80.9378-60">[60]</a></sup> Faith is not purely intellectual, but involves trust in God's promise to save.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001114_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001114-61">[61]</a></sup> Protestants do not hold there to be any other requirement for salvation, but that <a href="/wiki/Faith_alone" class="mw-redirect" title="Faith alone">faith alone</a> is sufficient.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201077.E2.80.9378_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201077.E2.80.9378-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">Justification</a> is the part of salvation where God pardons the sin of those who believe in Christ. It is historically held by Protestants to be the most important article of Christian faith, though more recently it is sometimes given less importance out of <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical">ecumenical</a> concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201080_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201080-62">[62]</a></sup> People are not on their own able even to fully <a href="/wiki/Repent" class="mw-redirect" title="Repent">repent</a> of their sin or prepare themselves to repent because of their sinfulness. Therefore, justification is held to arise solely from God's free and gracious act.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001113_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001113-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sanctification" title="Sanctification">Sanctification</a> is the part of salvation in which God makes the believer holy, by enabling them to exercise greater love for God and for other people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201084_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201084-64">[64]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Good_works" title="Good works">good works</a> accomplished by believers as they are sanctified are considered to be the necessary outworking of the believer's salvation, though they do not cause the believer to be saved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001114_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001114-61">[61]</a></sup> Sanctification, like justification, is by faith, because doing good works is simply living as the son of God one has become.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201085_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201085-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predestination">Predestination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Predestination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Predestination_in_Calvinism" title="Predestination in Calvinism">Predestination in Calvinism</a></div>
<p>Reformed theologians teach that sin so affects human nature that they are unable even to exercise faith in Christ by their own will. While people are said to retain will, in that they willfully sin, they are unable not to sin because of the corruption of their nature due to original sin. Reformed Christians believe that God <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestined</a> some people to be saved, which also means others are <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestined</a> to sin. This <a href="/wiki/Unconditional_election" title="Unconditional election">choice by God to save some and leave others is held to be unconditional</a> and not based on any characteristic or action on the part of the person chosen. This view is opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Arminian" class="mw-redirect" title="Arminian">Arminian</a> view that God's <a href="/wiki/Conditional_election" title="Conditional election">choice of whom to save is conditional</a> or based on his foreknowledge of who would respond positively to God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2010100.E2.80.93101_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2010100.E2.80.93101-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Karl Barth reinterpreted the Reformed doctrine of predestination to apply only to Christ. Individual people are only said to be elected through their being in Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001229.E2.80.93230_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKim2001229.E2.80.93230-67">[67]</a></sup> Reformed theologians who followed Barth, including <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Moltmann" title="Jürgen Moltmann">Jürgen Moltmann</a>, David Migliore, and <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Guthrie" title="Shirley Guthrie">Shirley Guthrie</a>, have argued that the traditional Reformed concept of predestination is speculative and have proposed alternative models. These theologians claim that a properly trinitarian doctrine emphasizes God's freedom to love all people, rather than choosing some for salvation and others for damnation. God's justice towards and condemnation of sinful people is spoken of by these theologians as out of his love for them and a desire to reconcile them to himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie200847.E2.80.9349_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie200847.E2.80.9349-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_points_of_Calvinism">Five points of Calvinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Five points of Calvinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width: 180px">
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em">The Five Points<br />
of <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Calvinism</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><a href="/wiki/File:Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg" class="image" title="A &quot;Page Polka&quot; tulip"><img alt="A &quot;Page Polka&quot; tulip" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg/50px-Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg" width="50" height="41" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg/75px-Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg/100px-Tulipa_classic_FotoTakkk_Hungary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2348" data-file-height="1904" /></a></td>
</tr>

</tr>

</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.1em"><a href="/wiki/Limited_atonement" title="Limited atonement">Limited atonement</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.1em"><a href="/wiki/Irresistible_grace" title="Irresistible grace">Irresistible grace</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.1em"><a href="/wiki/Perseverance_of_the_saints" title="Perseverance of the saints">Perseverance of the saints</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TULIP" title="Template:TULIP"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TULIP" title="Template talk:TULIP"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TULIP&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Most objections to and attacks on Calvinism focus on the "five points of Calvinism", also called the doctrines of grace, and remembered by the <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">mnemonic</a> "TULIP".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorton2011b15_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorton2011b15-69">[69]</a></sup> The five points are popularly said to summarize the <a href="/wiki/Canons_of_Dort" title="Canons of Dort">Canons of Dort</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> however, there is no historical relationship between them, and some scholars argue that their language distorts the meaning of the Canons, Calvin's theology, and the theology of 17th-century Calvinistic orthodoxy, particularly in the language of total depravity and limited atonement.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The five points were more recently popularized in the 1963 booklet <i>The Five Points of Calvinism Defined, Defended, Documented</i> by David N. Steele and Curtis C. Thomas. The origins of the five points and the acronym are uncertain, but they appear to be outlined in the <a href="/wiki/Counter_Remonstrance_of_1611" title="Counter Remonstrance of 1611">Counter Remonstrance of 1611</a>, a less known Reformed reply to the Arminians that occurred prior to the Canons of Dort.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The acronym was used by <a href="/wiki/Cleland_Boyd_McAfee" title="Cleland Boyd McAfee">Cleland Boyd McAfee</a> as early as circa 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> An early printed appearance of the T-U-L-I-P acronym is in Loraine Boettner's 1932 book, <i>The Reformed Doctrine of Predestination</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The acronym was very cautiously if ever used by Calvinist apologists and theologians before the booklet by Steele and Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> More recently, theologians have sought to reformulate the TULIP acronym to more accurately reflect the Canons of Dort.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The central assertion of these points is that God saves every person upon whom he has mercy, and that his efforts are not frustrated by the unrighteousness or inability of humans.</p>
<ul>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Total_depravity" title="Total depravity">Total depravity</a>", also called "total inability", asserts that as a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Man" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of Man">fall of man into sin</a>, every person is enslaved to sin. People are not by nature inclined to love God, but rather to serve their own interests and to reject the rule of God. Thus, all people by their own faculties are morally unable to choose to follow God and be saved (the term "total" in this context refers to sin affecting every part of a person, not that every person is as evil as they could be).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> This doctrine is derived from <a href="/wiki/Augustine" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustine">Augustine</a>'s explanation of <a href="/wiki/Original_Sin" class="mw-redirect" title="Original Sin">Original Sin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> While the phrases "totally depraved" and "utterly perverse" were used by Calvin, what was meant was the inability to save oneself from sin rather than being absent of goodness. Phrases like "total depravity" cannot be found in the Canons of Dort, and the Canons as well as later Reformed orthodox theologians arguably offer a more moderate view of the nature of fallen humanity than Calvin.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Unconditional_election" title="Unconditional election">Unconditional election</a>" asserts that God has chosen from eternity those whom he will bring to himself not based on foreseen virtue, merit, or faith in those people; rather, his choice is unconditionally grounded in his mercy alone. God has chosen from eternity to extend mercy to those he has chosen and to withhold mercy from those not chosen. Those chosen receive salvation through Christ alone. Those not chosen receive the just wrath that is warranted for their sins against God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWCF1646_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWCF1646-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Limited_atonement" title="Limited atonement">Limited atonement</a>", also called "particular redemption" or "definite atonement", asserts that Jesus's <a href="/wiki/Substitutionary_atonement" title="Substitutionary atonement">substitutionary atonement</a> was definite and certain in its purpose and in what it accomplished. This implies that only the sins of the elect were <a href="/wiki/Penal_substitution" title="Penal substitution">atoned</a> for by Jesus's death. Calvinists do not believe, however, that the atonement is limited in its value or power, but rather that the atonement is limited in the sense that it is intended for some and not all. Some Calvinists have summarized this as "The atonement is sufficient for all and efficient for the elect."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Irresistible_grace" title="Irresistible grace">Irresistible grace</a>", also called "efficacious grace", asserts that the saving grace of God is effectually applied to those whom he has determined to save (that is, the elect) and overcomes their resistance to obeying the call of the gospel, bringing them to a saving faith. This means that when God sovereignly purposes to save someone, that individual certainly will be saved. The doctrine holds that this purposeful influence of God's <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> cannot be resisted, but that the Holy Spirit, "graciously causes the elect sinner to cooperate, to believe, to repent, to come freely and willingly to Christ." This is not to deny the fact that the Spirit's outward call (through the proclamation of the Gospel) can be, and often is, rejected by sinners; rather, it's that inward call which cannot be rejected.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Perseverance_of_the_saints" title="Perseverance of the saints">Perseverance of the saints</a>" (also known as "perseverance of God with the saints" and "preservation of the believing") (the word "saints" is used to refer to all who are set apart by God, and not of those who are exceptionally <a href="/wiki/Holy" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy">holy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonized</a>, or in heaven) asserts that since God is sovereign and his will cannot be frustrated by humans or anything else, those whom God has called into communion with himself will continue in faith until the end. Those who apparently fall away either never had true faith to begin with (1 John 2:19), or, if they are saved but not presently walking in the Spirit, they will be divinely chastened (Hebrews 12:5–11) and will repent (1 John 3:6–9).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_among_Protestants">Comparison among Protestants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvinism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Comparison among Protestants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<center>
<table width="80%" cellpadding="5px" cellspacing="2" style="margin-bottom:1em; text-align:left; border:1px solid #444; margin:0 10px 0 10px;">

<td><b>Human will</b></td>

<td><a href="/wiki/Total_depravity" title="Total depravity">Total depravity</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-WELS-Compare_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WELS-Compare-84">[84]</a></sup> Humanity possesses free will in regard to "goods and possessions", but is sinful by nature and unable to contribute to its own salvation. <sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></td>
